authenticate() throws IOException {\\n251:         if (saslState != SaslState.REAUTH_PROCESS_HANDSHAKE) {\\n252:             if (netOutBuffer != null && }\\n500:     }\\n501: \\n502:     /**\\n503:      * @throws InvalidRequestException if the request is not in Kafka format or if the API key is invalid. Clients\n504:      * that support SASL without support for KIP-43 (e.g. Kafka Clients 0.9.x) are in the former bucket - the first\n505:      * packet such clients send is a GSSAPI token starting with 0x60.\n506:      */\n507:     private void handleKafkaRequest(byte[] requestBytes) throws IOException, AuthenticationException {\n508:         try {\n509:             ByteBuffer requestBuffer = ByteBuffer.wrap(requestBytes);\n510:             RequestHeader header = RequestHeader.parse(requestBuffer);\n511:             ApiKeys apiKey = header.apiKey();\n512: \n513:             // Raise an error prior to parsing if the api cannot be handled at this layer. This avoids\n514:             // unnecessary exposure to some of the more complex schema types.\n515:             if (apiKey != ApiKeys.API_VERSIONS && apiKey != ApiKeys.SASL_HANDSHAKE)\n516:                 throw new InvalidRequestException(\"Unexpected Kafka request of type \" + apiKey + \" during SASL handshake.\");\n517: \n518:             LOG.debug(\"Handling Kafka request {} during {}\", apiKey, reauthInfo.authenticationOrReauthenticationText());\n519: \n520:             RequestContext requestContext = new RequestContext(header, connectionId, clientAddress(), Optional.of(clientPort()),\n521:                     KafkaPrincipal.ANONYMOUS, listenerName, securityProtocol, ClientInformation.EMPTY, false);\n522:             RequestAndSize requestAndSize = requestContext.parseRequest(requestBuffer);\n523: \n524:             // A valid Kafka request was received, we can now update the sasl state\n525:             if (saslState == SaslState.INITIAL_REQUEST)\n526:                 setSaslState(SaslState.HANDSHAKE_OR_VERSIONS_REQUEST);\n527: \n528:             if (apiKey == ApiKeys.API_VERSIONS)\n529: createSaslServer(clientMechanism);\n534:                     setSaslState(SaslState.AUTHENTICATE);\n535:                 }\n536:             }\n537:         } catch (InvalidRequestException e) {\n538:             if (saslState == SaslState.INITIAL_REQUEST) {\n539:                 // InvalidRequestException is thrown if the request is not in Kafka format or if the API key is invalid.\n540:                 // If it's the initial request, this could be an ancient client (see method documentation for more details),\n541:                 // a client configured with the wrong security protocol or a non kafka-client altogether (eg http client).\n542:                 throw new InvalidRequestException(\"Invalid request, potential reasons: kafka client configured with the \" +\n543:                     \"wrong security protocol, it does not support KIP-43 or it is not a kafka client.\", e);\n544:             }\n545:             throw e;\n546:         }\n547:     }\n548: \n549:     private String   this.enableKafkaSaslAuthenticateHeaders = flag;\n570:     }\n571: \n572:     private void handleApiVersionsRequest(RequestContext context, ApiVersionsRequest apiVersionsRequest) throws IOException \n651:         /*\n652:          * We define the REAUTH_BAD_MECHANISM state because the failed re-authentication\n653:          * metric does not get updated if we send back an error immediately upon     long retvalSessionLifetimeMs = 0L;\n669:             long authenticationEndMs = time.milliseconds();\n670:             authenticationEndNanos = time.nanoseconds();\n671:             Long Authorizer implementation starts loading its metadata.</li>\\n51:  *   <li>Broker starts SocketServer to accept connections and process requests.</li>\\n52:  *   <li>For each listener, SocketServer waits for authorization metadata to be available in the\\n53:  *       authorizer before accepting connections. The future returned by {@link #start(AuthorizerServerInfo)}\\n54:  *       for each listener must return only when authorizer is ready to authorize requests on the listener.</li>\\n55:  *   <li>Broker accepts connections. For each connection, broker performs authentication and then accepts Kafka requests.\\n56:  *       For each request, broker invokes {@link #authorize(AuthorizableRequestContext, List)} to authorize\\n57:  *       actions performed by the request.</li>\\n58:  * </ol>\\n59:  *\\n60:  * Authorizer implementation class may optionally implement @{@link org.apache.kafka.common.Reconfigurable}\\n61:  * to enable dynamic reconfiguration without restarting the broker.\\n62:  * <p>Authorizer implementation class may also optionally implement {@link org.apache.kafka.common.metrics.Monitorable}\\n63:  * to enable the authorizer to register metrics. The following tags are automatically added to all metrics registered:\\n64:  * <code>config</code> set to <code>authorizer.class.name</code>, <code>class</code> set to the Authorizer class name,\\n65:  * and <code>role</code> set to either <code>broker</code> or <code>controller</code>.\\n66:  * <p>\\n67:  * <b>Threading model:</b>\\n68:  * <ul>\\n69:  *   <li>All authorizer operations including authorization and ACL updates must be thread-safe.</li>\\n70:  *   <li>ACL update methods are asynchronous. Implementations with low update latency may return a\\n71:  *       completed future using {@link java.util.concurrent.CompletableFuture#completedFuture(Object)}.\\n72:  *       This ensures that the request will be handled synchronously by the caller without using a\\n73:  *       purgatory to wait for the result. If ACL updates require remote communication which may block,\\n74:  *       return a future that is completed asynchronously when the remote operation completes. This enables\\n75:  *       the caller to process other requests on the request threads without blocking.</li>\\n76:  *   <li>Any threads or thread pools used for processing remote operations asynchronously can be started during\\n77:  *       {@link #start(AuthorizerServerInfo)}. These threads must be shutdown during {@link Authorizer#close()}.</li>\\n78:  * </ul>\\n79:  * </p>\\n80:  */\\n81: public interface Authorizer extends Configurable, Closeable {\\n82: \\n83:     /**\\n84:      * Starts loading authorization metadata and returns futures that can be used to wait until\\n85:      * metadata for authorizing requests on each listener is available. Each listener will be\\n86:      * started only after its metadata is available and authorizer is ready to start authorizing\\n87:      * requests on that listener.\\n88:      *\\n89:      * @param serverInfo Metadata for the broker including broker id and listener endpoints\\n90:      * @return CompletionStage for each endpoint that completes when authorizer is ready to\\n91:      *         start authorizing requests on that listener.\\n92:      */\\n93:     Map<Endpoint, ? extends CompletionStage<Void>> start(AuthorizerServerInfo serverInfo);\\n94: \\n95:     /**\\n96:      * Authorizes the specified action. Authorizer implementation starts loading its metadata.</li>\n51:  *   <li>Broker starts SocketServer to accept connections and process requests.</li>\n52:  *   <li>For each listener, SocketServer waits for authorization metadata to be available in the\n53:  *       authorizer before accepting connections. The future returned by {@link #start(AuthorizerServerInfo)}\n54:  *       for each listener must return only when authorizer is ready to authorize requests on the listener.</li>\n55:  *   <li>Broker accepts connections. For each connection, broker performs authentication and then accepts Kafka requests.\n56:  *       For each request, broker invokes {@link #authorize(AuthorizableRequestContext, List)} to authorize\n57:  *       actions performed by the request.</li>\n58:  * </ol>\n59:  *\n60:  * Authorizer implementation class may optionally implement @{@link org.apache.kafka.common.Reconfigurable}\n61:  * to enable dynamic reconfiguration without restarting the broker.\n62:  * <p>Authorizer implementation class may also optionally implement {@link org.apache.kafka.common.metrics.Monitorable}\n63:  * to enable the authorizer to register metrics. The following tags are automatically added to all metrics registered:\n64:  * <code>config</code> set to <code>authorizer.class.name</code>, <code>class</code> set to the Authorizer class name,\n65:  * and <code>role</code> set to either <code>broker</code> or <code>controller</code>.\n66:  * <p>\n67:  * <b>Threading model:</b>\n68:  * <ul>\n69:  *   <li>All authorizer operations including authorization and ACL updates must be thread-safe.</li>\n70:  *   <li>ACL update methods are asynchronous. Implementations with low update latency may return a\n71:  *       completed future using {@link java.util.concurrent.CompletableFuture#completedFuture(Object)}.\n72:  *       This ensures that the request will be handled synchronously by the caller without using a\n73:  *       purgatory to wait for the result. If ACL updates require remote communication which may block,\n74:  *       return a future that is completed asynchronously when the remote operation completes. This enables\n75:  *       the caller to process other requests on the request threads without blocking.</li>\n76:  *   <li>Any threads or thread pools used for processing remote operations asynchronously can be started during\n77:  *       {@link #start(AuthorizerServerInfo)}. These threads must be shutdown during {@link Authorizer#close()}.</li>\n78:  * </ul>\n79:  * </p>\n80:  */\n81: public interface Authorizer extends Configurable, Closeable {\n82: \n83:     /**\n84:      * Starts loading authorization metadata and returns futures that can be used to wait until\n85:      * metadata for authorizing requests on each listener is available. More efficient\n171:      *    implementations may be added in custom authorizers that directly access cached entries.\n172:      * 2. allowPatterns.get(PatternType.PREFIXED).add(binding.pattern().name());\n262:                     break;\n263:                 default:\n264:             }\n265:         }\n266: \n267:         if Continuing through the SCRAM protocol message parsing with regex validation, then the OAUTHBEARER mechanism that validates JWT tokens, and finally the authorization layer with KafkaPrincipal extraction and the Authorizer interface for ACL enforcement.\n\nLooking at the supporting infrastructure: JAAS configuration loading, credential caching for SCRAM, and the unsecured OAuthBearer validator for development environments.\n\nThe actual entry points are through `SaslServerAuthenticator.authenticate()` which receives raw untrusted bytes from the TCP connection, and `handleKafkaRequest()` which processes subsequent requests.\n\nThen there are mechanism-specific handlers: `PlainSaslServer` processes NUL-delimited credentials, `ScramSaslServer` handles the SCRAM protocol exchange, and `OAuthBearerSaslServer` validates bearer tokens, with `handleHandshakeRequest()` determining which mechanism the client selected.\n\nFor PLAIN specifically, the flow starts when `authenticate()` reads raw bytes from the transport layer, passes them to `evaluateResponse()` which decodes and splits on NUL delimiters to extract the username and password, then the callback handler validates these credentials against the JAAS configuration before building the final principal.\n\nFor SCRAM, the authentication happens over two rounds—first parsing the client's initial message to extract the username and fetch stored credentials, then in the second round parsing the final message, verifying the nonce, and validating the HMAC proof. Both flows ultimately produce a `KafkaPrincipal` that gets passed to the authorizer.\n\nOAUTHBEARER takes a different approach where the bearer token is parsed directly and its principal name becomes the authorization ID. For mechanism negotiation, the handshake reads the client's mechanism preference from the incoming request bytes.\n\nNow I'm looking at the channel creation architecture—the server and client builders instantiate a `SaslChannelBuilder` for SASL_PLAINTEXT or SASL_SSL, which then configures per-mechanism callback handlers and builds a `KafkaChannel` with...\n\nThe authentication state machine progresses through distinct phases: initial request handling, optional API version negotiation, SASL handshake, mechanism-specific token exchange, and finally completion or failure states. At the network layer, raw bytes come in through `NetworkReceive` from the TCP socket, with `saslAuthRequestMaxReceiveSize` capping the buffer size...\n\nNow I'm looking at how untrusted data enters the system. The mechanism name from the client's SaslHandshakeRequest gets validated against a server-configured whitelist, so there's no injection risk there. For PLAIN authentication, the raw bytes are decoded as UTF-8 and split on null bytes, with the authorization ID checked to prevent spoofing—though individual field lengths aren't explicitly limited beyond the overall receive buffer constraint.\n\nPassword comparison uses constant-time comparison to prevent timing attacks, but there's a fundamental risk: credentials travel in cleartext when using SASL_PLAINTEXT instead of SASL_SSL, creating an eavesdropping vulnerability. With SCRAM, the token parsing is more restrictive, using regex patterns to validate the client's first message, and the nonce is verified to prevent replays. The proof itself is checked with timing-safe comparison, but the credential lookup still relies on the client-supplied username.\n\nFor OAUTHBEARER tokens, the default handler is development-only and skips JWT signature validation entirely—it just trusts whatever token is provided. Production setups need to use the proper validator that actually checks signatures. The token's principal name comes directly from the JWT claims.\n\nWhen extracting the principal, the `SaslServerAuthenticator` delegates to a principal builder that processes the authentication context. cts the `authorizationId` from `SaslServer.getAuthorizationID()`, which is set within the mechanism's `evaluateResponse()` only after successful credential verification.\n- For GSSAPI, applies `KerberosShortNamer` rules to map full Kerberos names to short names. Misconfigured rules could map different principals to the same short name.\n- For SCRAM with delegation tokens: `principal.tokenAuthenticated(true)` is set based on SCRAM negotiated property, not re-verified.\n\n### 5. Authorization (ACL)\n- After successful SASL auth, requests are handled with the `KafkaPrincipal` as identity.\n- `Authorizer.authorize(requestContext, actions)` is called for each request.\n- `StandardAuthorizer` is the production implementation; it uses KRaft metadata for ACL storage.\n- The principal type defaults to `\"User\"` for all SASL mechanisms except custom `KafkaPrincipalBuilder` implementations.\n\n### 6. Re-authentication\n- Re-authentication is supported (KIP-368); it reuses the same SASL flow but starts from `REAUTH_PROCESS_HANDSHAKE`.\n- `ensurePrincipalUnchanged()` verifies that the re-authenticated principal matches the original. This prevents session hijacking during re-auth.\n\n### 7. Error Information Leakage\n- `SaslServerAuthenticator.handleSaslToken()` suppresses `SaslException` messages and returns a generic error instead, preventing username enumeration attacks.\n- `SaslAuthenticationException` is thrown explicitly in PlainSaslServer to handle authentication failures securely.\n\n### 8. Key Security Gaps\n\nI'm identifying several critical vulnerabilities: the OAuthBearer default handler skips JWT signature validation entirely, accepting self-signed tokens as a dangerous misconfiguration. PLAIN mechanism transmits credentials in cleartext over unencrypted transport without enforcing SASL_SSL. Custom `KafkaPrincipalBuilder` implementations are trusted implicitly, so a malicious builder could return arbitrary principals. Kafka SASL Authentication Flow Security Analysis\n\n## Files Examined\n\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/ChannelBuilders.java` — Factory for all channel builders; selects SASL/SSL/Plaintext based on `SecurityProtocol`; constructs `SaslChannelBuilder` with per-mechanism JAAS contexts\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/SaslChannelBuilder.java` — Wires up per-mechanism callback handlers (`PlainServerCallbackHandler`, `ScramServerCallbackHandler`, `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler`, `SaslServerCallbackHandler`); creates `SaslServerAuthenticator` via supplier lazy-evaluated on first connection\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/KafkaChannel.java` — Represents a single connection; delegates authentication to an `Authenticator` instance; calls `authenticate()` until `complete()` returns true before accepting normal requests\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/SaslServerAuthenticator.java` — Core server-side SASL state machine; reads raw bytes from transport layer; dispatches to `handleKafkaRequest()` or `handleSaslToken()` based on state; enforces receive-size limit (default 512 KB)\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/SaslClientAuthenticator.java` — Client-side SASL authenticator; sends ApiVersionsRequest, SaslHandshakeRequest, and mechanism-specific tokens\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/plain/internals/PlainSaslServer.java` — PLAIN mechanism server; parses NUL-delimited `[authzid]\\0authcid\\0passwd` message; delegates credential check to callback handler\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/plain/internals/PlainServerCallbackHandler.java` — Validates PLAIN credentials against JAAS config entries (`user_<username>` keys) using timing-safe comparison\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramSaslServer.java` — SCRAM-SHA-256/512 server; two-round challenge-response; nonce verification; cryptographic proof via HMAC; timing-safe stored-key comparison\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramServerCallbackHandler.java` — Looks up SCRAM credentials from in-memory `CredentialCache` (populated from ZooKeeper/KRaft); also handles delegation token lookup\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramMessages.java` — Parses SCRAM protocol messages using strict regex patterns; raises `SaslException` on format violations\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/OAuthBearerSaslServer.java` — OAUTHBEARER server; parses bearer token from client response; calls callback handler for JWT validation; uses `OAuthBearerToken.principalName()` as authorization ID\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/unsecured/OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler.java` — **Development-only** default OAuthBearer validator; validates only `exp`/`nbf`/`iat` timing claims; performs NO cryptographic signature verification; used automatically unless a production handler is explicitly configured\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java` — Extracts `KafkaPrincipal` from `SaslAuthenticationContext` using `SaslServer.getAuthorizationID()`; applies `KerberosShortNamer` for GSSAPI\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/auth/KafkaPrincipal.java` — Identity object used throughout authorization; carries `principalType` (\"User\") and `name` (the authenticated identity); also tracks `tokenAuthenticated` flag\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/server/authorizer/Authorizer.java` — Pluggable ACL authorization interface; `authorize(requestContext, actions)` called per-request after authentication completes\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/JaasContext.java` — Loads JAAS login module configuration; supports both static file-based and dynamic `sasl.jaas.config` property; blocks disallowed login modules\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/CredentialCache.java` — In-memory store for SCRAM credentials per mechanism; populated by AdminClient operations via ZooKeeper/KRaft metadata\n\n---\n\n## Entry Points\n\n1. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` (line 250) — accepts raw bytes from any TCP client connecting on a SASL listener; no authentication required at this point\n2. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleKafkaRequest()` (line 507) — parses untrusted bytes as Kafka request headers (`ApiVersionsRequest` or `SaslHandshakeRequest`); mechanism name string comes from client\n3. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleSaslToken()` (line 421) — accepts raw bytes for mechanism-specific SASL token exchange; routes to `SaslServer.evaluateResponse(clientToken)` without intermediate sanitization\n4. `PlainSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 71) — accepts NUL-delimited bytes containing authzid, username, and plaintext password from the untrusted client\n5. `ScramSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 96) — accepts two rounds of client-supplied SCRAM protocol bytes (`ClientFirstMessage` with username+nonce, `ClientFinalMessage` with proof)\n6. `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 86) — accepts raw OAuth bearer token bytes from client; parses token value and authorizationId\n7. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleHandshakeRequest()` (line 549) — reads client-supplied mechanism name string; validates against server-configured whitelist\n\n---\n\n## Data Flow\n\n### Flow 1: PLAIN Authentication (credential in cleartext token)\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` (line 264) — `netInBuffer.readFrom(transportLayer)` reads raw TCP bytes up to `saslAuthRequestMaxReceiveSize` (default 524,288 bytes)\n2. **Transform**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleSaslToken()` (line 461) — bytes passed directly to `saslServer.evaluateResponse(Utils.copyArray(saslAuthenticateRequest.data().authBytes()))` with no intermediate validation\n3. **Transform**: `PlainSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 85-89) — raw bytes decoded as UTF-8, split on NUL (`\\u0000`) into `[authzid, username, password]` tokens; empty username/password rejected (lines 91-96)\n4. **Callback**: `PlainServerCallbackHandler.java:authenticate()` (line 61-69) — constructs JAAS key `\"user_\" + username` and looks up expected password; comparison via `Utils.isEqualConstantTime()` (timing-safe)\n5. **Sink**: `PlainSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` (line 137) returns `authorizationId = username` → `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.build()` creates `KafkaPrincipal(\"User\", username)` → `Authorizer.authorize()` enforces ACLs\n\n### Flow 2: SCRAM-SHA-256 Authentication (multi-round challenge-response)\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` (line 264) — reads raw bytes from transport layer per round\n2. **Round 1 – ClientFirstMessage**: `ScramSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 100) — `new ClientFirstMessage(response)` parses bytes using regex `PATTERN` in `ScramMessages.java` (line 68-74); extracts `saslName`, `nonce`, optional `authorizationId`, and extensions; username resolved via `ScramFormatter.username(saslName)`\n3. **Credential Lookup**: `ScramServerCallbackHandler.java:handle()` (line 66-67) — `credentialCache.get(username)` fetches stored `ScramCredential` (salt, iterations, storedKey, serverKey); null credential → authentication fails\n4. **Server Response**: `ScramSaslServer.java` (line 135-138) — sends `ServerFirstMessage` with combined nonce (client nonce + server nonce), salt, and iteration count back to client\n5. **Round 2 – ClientFinalMessage**: `ScramSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 149) — parses `ClientFinalMessage`; verifies nonce matches (line 150-151); calls `verifyClientProof()` (line 153)\n6. **Proof Verification**: `ScramSaslServer.java:verifyClientProof()` (line 227-236) — `MessageDigest.isEqual(computedStoredKey, expectedStoredKey)` (timing-safe); `InvalidKeyException` on HMAC failure\n7. **Sink**: `ScramSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` returns `authorizationId = username` → `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.build()` → `KafkaPrincipal` → `Authorizer.authorize()`\n\n### Flow 3: OAUTHBEARER Authentication (JWT bearer token)\n\n1. **Source**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 95) — `new OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse(response)` parses client-supplied bytes; extracts `tokenValue`, `authorizationId`, and SASL extensions\n2. **Transform**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:process()` — calls `callbackHandler.handle(new Callback[]{validatorCallback, extensionsCallback})`\n3. **Default validation** (dev-only): `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` — decodes JWT without signature verification; checks `exp`/`nbf`/`iat` timestamps only; extracts principal name from a configurable claim (`sub` by default)\n4. **Sink**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` (line 108) returns `tokenForNegotiatedProperty.principalName()` — value comes entirely from client-supplied JWT payload → `KafkaPrincipal` → `Authorizer.authorize()`\n\n### Flow 4: Mechanism Negotiation (SaslHandshake)\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleKafkaRequest()` (line 509) — raw bytes parsed as `RequestHeader` then `SaslHandshakeRequest`\n2. **Transform**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleHandshakeRequest()` (line 550) — `clientMechanism = handshakeRequest.data().mechanism()` reads mechanism string from untrusted client\n3. **Validation**: `enabledMechanisms.contains(clientMechanism)` (line 554) — whitelist check against server-configured mechanisms; unsupported mechanisms return `UNSUPPORTED_SASL_MECHANISM` error\n4. **Decision**: if valid, `createSaslServer(clientMechanism)` (line 533) — creates the mechanism-specific `SaslServer` instance; state transitions to `AUTHENTICATE`\n\n### Flow 5: Principal to ACL Authorization\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:principal()` (line 307) — called after `saslServer.isComplete()` is true\n2. **Transform**: `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java:build()` (line 80-84) — creates `KafkaPrincipal(\"User\", saslServer.getAuthorizationID())`; for GSSAPI, applies `KerberosShortNamer` rules\n3. **Sink**: `Authorizer.java:authorize(requestContext, actions)` — receives `requestContext.principal()` which is the `KafkaPrincipal`; matches against ACL bindings by principal type + name + host + operation + resource\n\n---\n\n## Dependency Chain\n\n```\nTCP connection accepted on SASL listener\n  │\n  ▼\nChannelBuilders.serverChannelBuilder()\n  │  selects SecurityProtocol (SASL_PLAINTEXT / SASL_SSL)\n  │\n  ▼\nSaslChannelBuilder.configure()\n  │  loads JaasContext per mechanism\n  │  creates per-mechanism AuthenticateCallbackHandler\n  │  acquires LoginManager / Subject\n  │\n  ▼\nSaslChannelBuilder.buildChannel()\n  │  wraps SocketChannel in TransportLayer (Plaintext or SSL)\n  │  creates KafkaChannel with lazy SaslServerAuthenticator supplier\n  │\n  ▼\nSaslServerAuthenticator.authenticate()   ← raw bytes enter here\n  │\n  ├─[INITIAL_REQUEST / HANDSHAKE states]──►handleKafkaRequest()\n  │                                          │\n  │                                          ├─ ApiVersionsRequest ──► handleApiVersionsRequest()\n  │                                          │   registers ClientInformation (clientSoftwareName from client)\n  │                                          │\n  │                                          └─ SaslHandshakeRequest ──► handleHandshakeRequest()\n  │                                              mechanism whitelist check\n  │                                              createSaslServer(mechanism)\n  │\n  └─[AUTHENTICATE state]──────────────────►handleSaslToken()\n                                             │\n                                             └─ SaslServer.evaluateResponse(clientToken)\n                                                  │\n                                                  ├─[PLAIN]──► PlainSaslServer.evaluateResponse()\n                                                  │             UTF-8 decode + NUL split\n                                                  │             PlainServerCallbackHandler → JAAS lookup\n                                                  │\n                                                  ├─[SCRAM]──► ScramSaslServer.evaluateResponse()\n                                                  │             ClientFirstMessage regex parse\n                                                  │             ScramServerCallbackHandler → CredentialCache\n                                                  │             ClientFinalMessage nonce+proof verify\n                                                  │\n                                                  ├─[OAUTHBEARER]──► OAuthBearerSaslServer.evaluateResponse()\n                                                  │                   OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse parse\n                                                  │                   OAuthBearerValidatorCallback (or Unsecured)\n                                                  │\n                                                  └─[GSSAPI]──► JVM SaslServer (Kerberos GSS-API)\n                                                                Subject.doAs context\n                                                                KDC ticket validation\n\nSaslServerAuthenticator.principal()\n  │\n  ▼\nDefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.build(SaslAuthenticationContext)\n  │  saslServer.getAuthorizationID() → string identity\n  │  KerberosShortNamer (GSSAPI only)\n  │\n  ▼\nKafkaPrincipal(\"User\", name)\n  │\n  ▼\nAuthorizer.authorize(requestContext, actions)\n  │  matches KafkaPrincipal against ACL bindings\n  │  returns ALLOWED / DENIED per action\n  │\n  ▼\nRequest processed or rejected\n```\n\n---\n\n## Analysis\n\n### Architecture Summary\n\nThe Kafka SASL subsystem uses a layered architecture:\n\n1. **Channel layer** (`ChannelBuilders` → `SaslChannelBuilder`): Configures the security protocol and creates per-mechanism infrastructure from broker configuration (JAAS, callback handlers, login subjects).\n2. **Authentication layer** (`SaslServerAuthenticator`): Implements a strict state machine that gates all protocol access. Until authentication is `COMPLETE`, no normal Kafka API requests are processed.\n3. **Mechanism layer** (`PlainSaslServer`, `ScramSaslServer`, `OAuthBearerSaslServer`, GSSAPI): Pluggable implementations via `javax.security.sasl.SaslServer` interface; each receives raw bytes and is responsible for its own protocol parsing and credential verification.\n4. **Callback layer** (`PlainServerCallbackHandler`, `ScramServerCallbackHandler`): Decouples credential storage from protocol logic, enabling custom credential backends.\n5. **Principal/Authorization layer** (`DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder` → `Authorizer`): Converts authenticated identity to a principal and enforces ACL-based access control.\n\n---\n\n### Vulnerability Class: Insufficient Input Validation at Mechanism Boundary\n\n**Severity: Informational to Medium (context-dependent)**\n\nAll untrusted data ultimately enters `SaslServer.evaluateResponse(byte[])`. The mechanism implementations perform their own parsing:\n- PLAIN: UTF-8 decode + NUL-character split (no length limits per field, only aggregate 512 KB limit)\n- SCRAM: Regex-validated structured message (well-defined grammar via `ScramMessages.java`)\n- OAUTHBEARER: `OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse` parses a structured message format\n\nNo cross-mechanism validation is done before dispatch; the state machine trusts that once `createSaslServer(mechanism)` selects a mechanism, all subsequent bytes belong to that mechanism's protocol.\n\n---\n\n### Critical Gap: OAuthBearer Default Handler (CVE-class: Authentication Bypass)\n\n**Severity: Critical in OAuthBearer deployments without explicit handler configuration**\n\n`SaslChannelBuilder.createServerCallbackHandlers()` (line 330-331):\n```java\nelse if (mechanism.equals(OAuthBearerLoginModule.OAUTHBEARER_MECHANISM))\n    callbackHandler = new OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler();\n```\n\nWhen `OAUTHBEARER` is in `sasl.enabled.mechanisms` but no `sasl.oauthbearer.jwks.endpoint.url` or custom `sasl.server.callback.handler.class` is configured, the broker silently uses `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler`. This handler:\n- Does **not** verify JWT signatures (no HMAC or RSA/EC public key validation)\n- Accepts any well-formed JWT where `exp` is in the future\n- Extracts the principal name from the `sub` claim (or configurable claim) in the **client-supplied** JWT payload\n\n**Attack scenario**: An unauthenticated attacker can craft an arbitrary JWT with `exp` set to the future and any desired `sub` claim value (e.g., `\"admin\"` or any super-user name), base64-encode it with no signature, and authenticate as any principal. This bypasses all SASL authentication.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: The documentation (`OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler.java`, lines 79-80) states it \"is not suitable for production use.\" However, this is only a documentation warning; the code does not prevent use in production or emit a broker startup warning.\n\n**Recommended remediation**:\n- Emit a `WARN` log at broker startup if `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` is active\n- Consider refusing to start if `OAUTHBEARER` is enabled without a production-grade callback handler\n- Document mandatory configuration of `sasl.oauthbearer.jwks.endpoint.url` for OAUTHBEARER in all deployment guides\n\n---\n\n### Gap: PLAIN Mechanism Over Unencrypted Transport\n\n**Severity: High in SASL_PLAINTEXT deployments**\n\n`PlainSaslServer.evaluateResponse()` transmits the password in cleartext as part of the SASL token. When used with `SASL_PLAINTEXT` (not `SASL_SSL`), these credentials traverse the network unencrypted.\n\nThe code does not enforce that PLAIN is only used over TLS. The `PlainSaslServer` does not inspect the `SecurityProtocol` or transport-layer encryption state.\n\n**Attack scenario**: A network eavesdropper on a SASL_PLAINTEXT listener can capture any connection establishment and extract plaintext credentials from the SASL token.\n\n**Existing mitigations**: Kafka documentation recommends using `SASL_SSL` for production. The separation of `SASL_PLAINTEXT` and `SASL_SSL` security protocols is enforced at the `ChannelBuilders` level.\n\n**Recommended remediation**: Add a broker startup check that rejects `PLAIN` mechanism configuration on `SASL_PLAINTEXT` listeners, or log a prominent warning.\n\n---\n\n### Gap: ClientInformation Stored Pre-Authentication (Minor Information Leak)\n\n**Severity: Low**\n\n`SaslServerAuthenticator.handleApiVersionsRequest()` (line 581-582):\n```java\nmetadataRegistry.registerClientInformation(new ClientInformation(\n    apiVersionsRequest.data().clientSoftwareName(),\n    apiVersionsRequest.data().clientSoftwareVersion()));\n```\n\nThe `ApiVersionsRequest` is processed **before** authentication completes, and the client-supplied `clientSoftwareName` and `clientSoftwareVersion` strings are stored in `ChannelMetadataRegistry`. These values are unauthenticated and could contain arbitrary strings from an attacker.\n\n**Attack scenario**: If `ClientInformation` is exposed in metrics, logs, or admin APIs, an attacker can inject arbitrary strings for tracking/confusion purposes. This is not an authentication bypass but affects observability integrity.\n\n---\n\n### Existing Mitigations\n\n| Control | Location | Effectiveness |\n|---|---|---|\n| Receive size limit (512 KB default) | `SaslServerAuthenticator` line 195-197 | Prevents OOM from oversized auth payloads |\n| Mechanism whitelist | `handleHandshakeRequest()` line 554 | Prevents use of server-unsupported mechanisms |\n| Timing-safe password comparison | `PlainServerCallbackHandler.java` line 68 | Prevents timing-based username enumeration for PLAIN |\n| Timing-safe SCRAM proof comparison | `ScramSaslServer.java` line 232 | Prevents timing-based secret extraction for SCRAM |\n| Error message sanitization | `SaslServerAuthenticator.java` line 487-496 | Prevents leaking backend error details (SaslException vs SaslAuthenticationException distinction) |\n| SCRAM minimum iterations check | `ScramSaslServer.java` line 133-134 | Enforces minimum work factor |\n| Re-authentication principal check | `SaslServerAuthenticator.java` line 427, 463-464 | Prevents session hijacking during re-auth |\n| Nonce freshness check (SCRAM) | `ScramSaslServer.java` line 150-151 | Prevents replay attacks |\n| JAAS login module blocklist | `JaasContext.java` line 100 | Blocks dangerous login modules |\n| Mechanism-mechanism isolation | State machine in `SaslServerAuthenticator` | One mechanism per connection; no cross-mechanism interference |\n\n---\n\n### Attack Scenarios Summary\n\n**Scenario 1 – OAuthBearer Bypass (Critical)**\n- Precondition: Broker configured with `OAUTHBEARER` in `sasl.enabled.mechanisms`, no custom callback handler\n- Steps: Client crafts JWT `{\"alg\":\"none\"}{\"sub\":\"admin\",\"exp\":<future>}`, base64-encodes with empty signature, sends as OAUTHBEARER token\n- Result: Authenticates as principal `User:admin`; full ACL access if admin ACLs exist or no ACLs are configured\n\n**Scenario 2 – Credential Eavesdropping (PLAIN + SASL_PLAINTEXT)**\n- Precondition: Broker using SASL_PLAINTEXT with PLAIN mechanism (common in internal/development clusters)\n- Steps: Attacker with network access captures TCP stream during client authentication\n- Result: Obtains plaintext username and password directly from the SASL token\n\n**Scenario 3 – Username Enumeration via SCRAM (Mitigated but partial)**\n- Precondition: SCRAM enabled, attacker can observe authentication response timing\n- Steps: Send ClientFirstMessage for valid vs. invalid usernames\n- Partially mitigated: Null credential returns `SaslException` with generic message; BUT the `ScramServerCallbackHandler` returns null from `credentialCache.get(username)` which may have subtly different timing than a found credential (cache miss vs. hit). The timing-safe comparison is only invoked in `verifyClientProof()` during round 2, which is never reached for unknown users.\n- Result: Potential username oracle via timing difference between unknown (fails in round 1) vs. known (proceeds to round 2) users\n\n**Scenario 4 – SASL State Machine Confusion**\n- Precondition: Attacker sends unexpected API key during AUTHENTICATE state\n- Behavior: `handleSaslToken()` line 440-443 returns an error response for non-`SASL_AUTHENTICATE` API keys; throws `IllegalSaslStateException` which transitions to `FAILED` state\n- Result: Connection terminated; no authentication bypass; well-handled\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nThe Kafka SASL authentication subsystem implements a sound architectural foundation: a strict state machine gates all requests until authentication completes, mechanism dispatch uses a server-side whitelist, and credential verification uses timing-safe comparisons. However, two significant gaps exist in the current implementation.\n\n**The most critical issue** is the default use of `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` for OAUTHBEARER deployments without explicit production configuration. This handler performs no JWT signature verification, allowing any attacker to craft a token claiming any principal identity and authenticate without valid credentials. A broker can be silently misconfigured into this vulnerable state simply by enabling OAUTHBEARER without specifying a production callback handler.\n\n**The second significant issue** is the absence of enforcement preventing PLAIN authentication over unencrypted `SASL_PLAINTEXT` transport, exposing credentials to network eavesdroppers in misconfigured deployments.\n\nThe SCRAM implementation is cryptographically sound (HMAC-SHA-256/512, timing-safe comparison, nonce-based replay protection, minimum iteration enforcement), but introduces a partial username oracle due to authentication failing at round 1 for unknown users versus round 2 for known users with incorrect passwords.\n\nThe full path from untrusted input to authorized principal is:\n`TCP socket bytes → NetworkReceive → SaslServerAuthenticator → SaslServer.evaluateResponse() → [mechanism parsing + callback handler credential lookup] → SaslServer.getAuthorizationID() → DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder → KafkaPrincipal → Authorizer.authorize()`\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":3,"cache_creation_input_tokens":391,"cache_read_input_tokens":82418,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":391,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":1,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"d37f1f18-16fe-45f6-a020-973114f39862","uuid":"a661aeae-3f73-4e34-8281-2bd4717dd691"}

Kafka SASL Authentication Flow Security Analysis\n\n## Files Examined\n\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/ChannelBuilders.java` — Factory for all channel builders; selects SASL/SSL/Plaintext based on `SecurityProtocol`; constructs `SaslChannelBuilder` with per-mechanism JAAS contexts\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/SaslChannelBuilder.java` — Wires up per-mechanism callback handlers (`PlainServerCallbackHandler`, `ScramServerCallbackHandler`, `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler`, `SaslServerCallbackHandler`); creates `SaslServerAuthenticator` via supplier lazy-evaluated on first connection\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/KafkaChannel.java` — Represents a single connection; delegates authentication to an `Authenticator` instance; calls `authenticate()` until `complete()` returns true before accepting normal requests\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/SaslServerAuthenticator.java` — Core server-side SASL state machine; reads raw bytes from transport layer; dispatches to `handleKafkaRequest()` or `handleSaslToken()` based on state; enforces receive-size limit (default 512 KB)\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/SaslClientAuthenticator.java` — Client-side SASL authenticator; sends ApiVersionsRequest, SaslHandshakeRequest, and mechanism-specific tokens\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/plain/internals/PlainSaslServer.java` — PLAIN mechanism server; parses NUL-delimited `[authzid]\\0authcid\\0passwd` message; delegates credential check to callback handler\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/plain/internals/PlainServerCallbackHandler.java` — Validates PLAIN credentials against JAAS config entries (`user_<username>` keys) using timing-safe comparison\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramSaslServer.java` — SCRAM-SHA-256/512 server; two-round challenge-response; nonce verification; cryptographic proof via HMAC; timing-safe stored-key comparison\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramServerCallbackHandler.java` — Looks up SCRAM credentials from in-memory `CredentialCache` (populated from ZooKeeper/KRaft); also handles delegation token lookup\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramMessages.java` — Parses SCRAM protocol messages using strict regex patterns; raises `SaslException` on format violations\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/OAuthBearerSaslServer.java` — OAUTHBEARER server; parses bearer token from client response; calls callback handler for JWT validation; uses `OAuthBearerToken.principalName()` as authorization ID\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/unsecured/OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler.java` — **Development-only** default OAuthBearer validator; validates only `exp`/`nbf`/`iat` timing claims; performs NO cryptographic signature verification; used automatically unless a production handler is explicitly configured\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java` — Extracts `KafkaPrincipal` from `SaslAuthenticationContext` using `SaslServer.getAuthorizationID()`; applies `KerberosShortNamer` for GSSAPI\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/auth/KafkaPrincipal.java` — Identity object used throughout authorization; carries `principalType` (\"User\") and `name` (the authenticated identity); also tracks `tokenAuthenticated` flag\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/server/authorizer/Authorizer.java` — Pluggable ACL authorization interface; `authorize(requestContext, actions)` called per-request after authentication completes\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/JaasContext.java` — Loads JAAS login module configuration; supports both static file-based and dynamic `sasl.jaas.config` property; blocks disallowed login modules\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/CredentialCache.java` — In-memory store for SCRAM credentials per mechanism; populated by AdminClient operations via ZooKeeper/KRaft metadata\n\n---\n\n## Entry Points\n\n1. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` (line 250) — accepts raw bytes from any TCP client connecting on a SASL listener; no authentication required at this point\n2. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleKafkaRequest()` (line 507) — parses untrusted bytes as Kafka request headers (`ApiVersionsRequest` or `SaslHandshakeRequest`); mechanism name string comes from client\n3. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleSaslToken()` (line 421) — accepts raw bytes for mechanism-specific SASL token exchange; routes to `SaslServer.evaluateResponse(clientToken)` without intermediate sanitization\n4. `PlainSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 71) — accepts NUL-delimited bytes containing authzid, username, and plaintext password from the untrusted client\n5. `ScramSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 96) — accepts two rounds of client-supplied SCRAM protocol bytes (`ClientFirstMessage` with username+nonce, `ClientFinalMessage` with proof)\n6. `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 86) — accepts raw OAuth bearer token bytes from client; parses token value and authorizationId\n7. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleHandshakeRequest()` (line 549) — reads client-supplied mechanism name string; validates against server-configured whitelist\n\n---\n\n## Data Flow\n\n### Flow 1: PLAIN Authentication (credential in cleartext token)\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` (line 264) — `netInBuffer.readFrom(transportLayer)` reads raw TCP bytes up to `saslAuthRequestMaxReceiveSize` (default 524,288 bytes)\n2. **Transform**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleSaslToken()` (line 461) — bytes passed directly to `saslServer.evaluateResponse(Utils.copyArray(saslAuthenticateRequest.data().authBytes()))` with no intermediate validation\n3. **Transform**: `PlainSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 85-89) — raw bytes decoded as UTF-8, split on NUL (`\\u0000`) into `[authzid, username, password]` tokens; empty username/password rejected (lines 91-96)\n4. **Callback**: `PlainServerCallbackHandler.java:authenticate()` (line 61-69) — constructs JAAS key `\"user_\" + username` and looks up expected password; comparison via `Utils.isEqualConstantTime()` (timing-safe)\n5. **Sink**: `PlainSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` (line 137) returns `authorizationId = username` → `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.build()` creates `KafkaPrincipal(\"User\", username)` → `Authorizer.authorize()` enforces ACLs\n\n### Flow 2: SCRAM-SHA-256 Authentication (multi-round challenge-response)\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` (line 264) — reads raw bytes from transport layer per round\n2. **Round 1 – ClientFirstMessage**: `ScramSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 100) — `new ClientFirstMessage(response)` parses bytes using regex `PATTERN` in `ScramMessages.java` (line 68-74); extracts `saslName`, `nonce`, optional `authorizationId`, and extensions; username resolved via `ScramFormatter.username(saslName)`\n3. **Credential Lookup**: `ScramServerCallbackHandler.java:handle()` (line 66-67) — `credentialCache.get(username)` fetches stored `ScramCredential` (salt, iterations, storedKey, serverKey); null credential → authentication fails\n4. **Server Response**: `ScramSaslServer.java` (line 135-138) — sends `ServerFirstMessage` with combined nonce (client nonce + server nonce), salt, and iteration count back to client\n5. **Round 2 – ClientFinalMessage**: `ScramSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 149) — parses `ClientFinalMessage`; verifies nonce matches (line 150-151); calls `verifyClientProof()` (line 153)\n6. **Proof Verification**: `ScramSaslServer.java:verifyClientProof()` (line 227-236) — `MessageDigest.isEqual(computedStoredKey, expectedStoredKey)` (timing-safe); `InvalidKeyException` on HMAC failure\n7. **Sink**: `ScramSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` returns `authorizationId = username` → `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.build()` → `KafkaPrincipal` → `Authorizer.authorize()`\n\n### Flow 3: OAUTHBEARER Authentication (JWT bearer token)\n\n1. **Source**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 95) — `new OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse(response)` parses client-supplied bytes; extracts `tokenValue`, `authorizationId`, and SASL extensions\n2. **Transform**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:process()` — calls `callbackHandler.handle(new Callback[]{validatorCallback, extensionsCallback})`\n3. **Default validation** (dev-only): `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` — decodes JWT without signature verification; checks `exp`/`nbf`/`iat` timestamps only; extracts principal name from a configurable claim (`sub` by default)\n4. **Sink**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` (line 108) returns `tokenForNegotiatedProperty.principalName()` — value comes entirely from client-supplied JWT payload → `KafkaPrincipal` → `Authorizer.authorize()`\n\n### Flow 4: Mechanism Negotiation (SaslHandshake)\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleKafkaRequest()` (line 509) — raw bytes parsed as `RequestHeader` then `SaslHandshakeRequest`\n2. **Transform**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleHandshakeRequest()` (line 550) — `clientMechanism = handshakeRequest.data().mechanism()` reads mechanism string from untrusted client\n3. **Validation**: `enabledMechanisms.contains(clientMechanism)` (line 554) — whitelist check against server-configured mechanisms; unsupported mechanisms return `UNSUPPORTED_SASL_MECHANISM` error\n4. **Decision**: if valid, `createSaslServer(clientMechanism)` (line 533) — creates the mechanism-specific `SaslServer` instance; state transitions to `AUTHENTICATE`\n\n### Flow 5: Principal to ACL Authorization\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:principal()` (line 307) — called after `saslServer.isComplete()` is true\n2. **Transform**: `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java:build()` (line 80-84) — creates `KafkaPrincipal(\"User\", saslServer.getAuthorizationID())`; for GSSAPI, applies `KerberosShortNamer` rules\n3. **Sink**: `Authorizer.java:authorize(requestContext, actions)` — receives `requestContext.principal()` which is the `KafkaPrincipal`; matches against ACL bindings by principal type + name + host + operation + resource\n\n---\n\n## Dependency Chain\n\n```\nTCP connection accepted on SASL listener\n  │\n  ▼\nChannelBuilders.serverChannelBuilder()\n  │  selects SecurityProtocol (SASL_PLAINTEXT / SASL_SSL)\n  │\n  ▼\nSaslChannelBuilder.configure()\n  │  loads JaasContext per mechanism\n  │  creates per-mechanism AuthenticateCallbackHandler\n  │  acquires LoginManager / Subject\n  │\n  ▼\nSaslChannelBuilder.buildChannel()\n  │  wraps SocketChannel in TransportLayer (Plaintext or SSL)\n  │  creates KafkaChannel with lazy SaslServerAuthenticator supplier\n  │\n  ▼\nSaslServerAuthenticator.authenticate()   ← raw bytes enter here\n  │\n  ├─[INITIAL_REQUEST / HANDSHAKE states]──►handleKafkaRequest()\n  │                                          │\n  │                                          ├─ ApiVersionsRequest ──► handleApiVersionsRequest()\n  │                                          │   registers ClientInformation (clientSoftwareName from client)\n  │                                          │\n  │                                          └─ SaslHandshakeRequest ──► handleHandshakeRequest()\n  │                                              mechanism whitelist check\n  │                                              createSaslServer(mechanism)\n  │\n  └─[AUTHENTICATE state]──────────────────►handleSaslToken()\n                                             │\n                                             └─ SaslServer.evaluateResponse(clientToken)\n                                                  │\n                                                  ├─[PLAIN]──► PlainSaslServer.evaluateResponse()\n                                                  │             UTF-8 decode + NUL split\n                                                  │             PlainServerCallbackHandler → JAAS lookup\n                                                  │\n                                                  ├─[SCRAM]──► ScramSaslServer.evaluateResponse()\n                                                  │             ClientFirstMessage regex parse\n                                                  │             ScramServerCallbackHandler → CredentialCache\n                                                  │             ClientFinalMessage nonce+proof verify\n                                                  │\n                                                  ├─[OAUTHBEARER]──► OAuthBearerSaslServer.evaluateResponse()\n                                                  │                   OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse parse\n                                                  │                   OAuthBearerValidatorCallback (or Unsecured)\n                                                  │\n                                                  └─[GSSAPI]──► JVM SaslServer (Kerberos GSS-API)\n                                                                Subject.doAs context\n                                                                KDC ticket validation\n\nSaslServerAuthenticator.principal()\n  │\n  ▼\nDefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.build(SaslAuthenticationContext)\n  │  saslServer.getAuthorizationID() → string identity\n  │  KerberosShortNamer (GSSAPI only)\n  │\n  ▼\nKafkaPrincipal(\"User\", name)\n  │\n  ▼\nAuthorizer.authorize(requestContext, actions)\n  │  matches KafkaPrincipal against ACL bindings\n  │  returns ALLOWED / DENIED per action\n  │\n  ▼\nRequest processed or rejected\n```\n\n---\n\n## Analysis\n\n### Architecture Summary\n\nThe Kafka SASL subsystem uses a layered architecture:\n\n1. **Channel layer** (`ChannelBuilders` → `SaslChannelBuilder`): Configures the security protocol and creates per-mechanism infrastructure from broker configuration (JAAS, callback handlers, login subjects).\n2. **Authentication layer** (`SaslServerAuthenticator`): Implements a strict state machine that gates all protocol access. Until authentication is `COMPLETE`, no normal Kafka API requests are processed.\n3. **Mechanism layer** (`PlainSaslServer`, `ScramSaslServer`, `OAuthBearerSaslServer`, GSSAPI): Pluggable implementations via `javax.security.sasl.SaslServer` interface; each receives raw bytes and is responsible for its own protocol parsing and credential verification.\n4. **Callback layer** (`PlainServerCallbackHandler`, `ScramServerCallbackHandler`): Decouples credential storage from protocol logic, enabling custom credential backends.\n5. **Principal/Authorization layer** (`DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder` → `Authorizer`): Converts authenticated identity to a principal and enforces ACL-based access control.\n\n---\n\n### Vulnerability Class: Insufficient Input Validation at Mechanism Boundary\n\n**Severity: Informational to Medium (context-dependent)**\n\nAll untrusted data ultimately enters `SaslServer.evaluateResponse(byte[])`. The mechanism implementations perform their own parsing:\n- PLAIN: UTF-8 decode + NUL-character split (no length limits per field, only aggregate 512 KB limit)\n- SCRAM: Regex-validated structured message (well-defined grammar via `ScramMessages.java`)\n- OAUTHBEARER: `OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse` parses a structured message format\n\nNo cross-mechanism validation is done before dispatch; the state machine trusts that once `createSaslServer(mechanism)` selects a mechanism, all subsequent bytes belong to that mechanism's protocol.\n\n---\n\n### Critical Gap: OAuthBearer Default Handler (CVE-class: Authentication Bypass)\n\n**Severity: Critical in OAuthBearer deployments without explicit handler configuration**\n\n`SaslChannelBuilder.createServerCallbackHandlers()` (line 330-331):\n```java\nelse if (mechanism.equals(OAuthBearerLoginModule.OAUTHBEARER_MECHANISM))\n    callbackHandler = new OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler();\n```\n\nWhen `OAUTHBEARER` is in `sasl.enabled.mechanisms` but no `sasl.oauthbearer.jwks.endpoint.url` or custom `sasl.server.callback.handler.class` is configured, the broker silently uses `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler`. This handler:\n- Does **not** verify JWT signatures (no HMAC or RSA/EC public key validation)\n- Accepts any well-formed JWT where `exp` is in the future\n- Extracts the principal name from the `sub` claim (or configurable claim) in the **client-supplied** JWT payload\n\n**Attack scenario**: An unauthenticated attacker can craft an arbitrary JWT with `exp` set to the future and any desired `sub` claim value (e.g., `\"admin\"` or any super-user name), base64-encode it with no signature, and authenticate as any principal. This bypasses all SASL authentication.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: The documentation (`OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler.java`, lines 79-80) states it \"is not suitable for production use.\" However, this is only a documentation warning; the code does not prevent use in production or emit a broker startup warning.\n\n**Recommended remediation**:\n- Emit a `WARN` log at broker startup if `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` is active\n- Consider refusing to start if `OAUTHBEARER` is enabled without a production-grade callback handler\n- Document mandatory configuration of `sasl.oauthbearer.jwks.endpoint.url` for OAUTHBEARER in all deployment guides\n\n---\n\n### Gap: PLAIN Mechanism Over Unencrypted Transport\n\n**Severity: High in SASL_PLAINTEXT deployments**\n\n`PlainSaslServer.evaluateResponse()` transmits the password in cleartext as part of the SASL token. When used with `SASL_PLAINTEXT` (not `SASL_SSL`), these credentials traverse the network unencrypted.\n\nThe code does not enforce that PLAIN is only used over TLS. The `PlainSaslServer` does not inspect the `SecurityProtocol` or transport-layer encryption state.\n\n**Attack scenario**: A network eavesdropper on a SASL_PLAINTEXT listener can capture any connection establishment and extract plaintext credentials from the SASL token.\n\n**Existing mitigations**: Kafka documentation recommends using `SASL_SSL` for production. The separation of `SASL_PLAINTEXT` and `SASL_SSL` security protocols is enforced at the `ChannelBuilders` level.\n\n**Recommended remediation**: Add a broker startup check that rejects `PLAIN` mechanism configuration on `SASL_PLAINTEXT` listeners, or log a prominent warning.\n\n---\n\n### Gap: ClientInformation Stored Pre-Authentication (Minor Information Leak)\n\n**Severity: Low**\n\n`SaslServerAuthenticator.handleApiVersionsRequest()` (line 581-582):\n```java\nmetadataRegistry.registerClientInformation(new ClientInformation(\n    apiVersionsRequest.data().clientSoftwareName(),\n    apiVersionsRequest.data().clientSoftwareVersion()));\n```\n\nThe `ApiVersionsRequest` is processed **before** authentication completes, and the client-supplied `clientSoftwareName` and `clientSoftwareVersion` strings are stored in `ChannelMetadataRegistry`. These values are unauthenticated and could contain arbitrary strings from an attacker.\n\n**Attack scenario**: If `ClientInformation` is exposed in metrics, logs, or admin APIs, an attacker can inject arbitrary strings for tracking/confusion purposes. This is not an authentication bypass but affects observability integrity.\n\n---\n\n### Existing Mitigations\n\n| Control | Location | Effectiveness |\n|---|---|---|\n| Receive size limit (512 KB default) | `SaslServerAuthenticator` line 195-197 | Prevents OOM from oversized auth payloads |\n| Mechanism whitelist | `handleHandshakeRequest()` line 554 | Prevents use of server-unsupported mechanisms |\n| Timing-safe password comparison | `PlainServerCallbackHandler.java` line 68 | Prevents timing-based username enumeration for PLAIN |\n| Timing-safe SCRAM proof comparison | `ScramSaslServer.java` line 232 | Prevents timing-based secret extraction for SCRAM |\n| Error message sanitization | `SaslServerAuthenticator.java` line 487-496 | Prevents leaking backend error details (SaslException vs SaslAuthenticationException distinction) |\n| SCRAM minimum iterations check | `ScramSaslServer.java` line 133-134 | Enforces minimum work factor |\n| Re-authentication principal check | `SaslServerAuthenticator.java` line 427, 463-464 | Prevents session hijacking during re-auth |\n| Nonce freshness check (SCRAM) | `ScramSaslServer.java` line 150-151 | Prevents replay attacks |\n| JAAS login module blocklist | `JaasContext.java` line 100 | Blocks dangerous login modules |\n| Mechanism-mechanism isolation | State machine in `SaslServerAuthenticator` | One mechanism per connection; no cross-mechanism interference |\n\n---\n\n### Attack Scenarios Summary\n\n**Scenario 1 – OAuthBearer Bypass (Critical)**\n- Precondition: Broker configured with `OAUTHBEARER` in `sasl.enabled.mechanisms`, no custom callback handler\n- Steps: Client crafts JWT `{\"alg\":\"none\"}{\"sub\":\"admin\",\"exp\":<future>}`, base64-encodes with empty signature, sends as OAUTHBEARER token\n- Result: Authenticates as principal `User:admin`; full ACL access if admin ACLs exist or no ACLs are configured\n\n**Scenario 2 – Credential Eavesdropping (PLAIN + SASL_PLAINTEXT)**\n- Precondition: Broker using SASL_PLAINTEXT with PLAIN mechanism (common in internal/development clusters)\n- Steps: Attacker with network access captures TCP stream during client authentication\n- Result: Obtains plaintext username and password directly from the SASL token\n\n**Scenario 3 – Username Enumeration via SCRAM (Mitigated but partial)**\n- Precondition: SCRAM enabled, attacker can observe authentication response timing\n- Steps: Send ClientFirstMessage for valid vs. invalid usernames\n- Partially mitigated: Null credential returns `SaslException` with generic message; BUT the `ScramServerCallbackHandler` returns null from `credentialCache.get(username)` which may have subtly different timing than a found credential (cache miss vs. hit). The timing-safe comparison is only invoked in `verifyClientProof()` during round 2, which is never reached for unknown users.\n- Result: Potential username oracle via timing difference between unknown (fails in round 1) vs. known (proceeds to round 2) users\n\n**Scenario 4 – SASL State Machine Confusion**\n- Precondition: Attacker sends unexpected API key during AUTHENTICATE state\n- Behavior: `handleSaslToken()` line 440-443 returns an error response for non-`SASL_AUTHENTICATE` API keys; throws `IllegalSaslStateException` which transitions to `FAILED` state\n- Result: Connection terminated; no authentication bypass; well-handled\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nThe Kafka SASL authentication subsystem implements a sound architectural foundation: a strict state machine gates all requests until authentication completes, mechanism dispatch uses a server-side whitelist, and credential verification uses timing-safe comparisons. However, two significant gaps exist in the current implementation.\n\n**The most critical issue** is the default use of `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` for OAUTHBEARER deployments without explicit production configuration. This handler performs no JWT signature verification, allowing any attacker to craft a token claiming any principal identity and authenticate without valid credentials. A broker can be silently misconfigured into this vulnerable state simply by enabling OAUTHBEARER without specifying a production callback handler.\n\n**The second significant issue** is the absence of enforcement preventing PLAIN authentication over unencrypted `SASL_PLAINTEXT` transport, exposing credentials to network eavesdroppers in misconfigured deployments.\n\nThe SCRAM implementation is cryptographically sound (HMAC-SHA-256/512, timing-safe comparison, nonce-based replay protection, minimum iteration enforcement), but introduces a partial username oracle due to authentication failing at round 1 for unknown users versus round 2 for known users with incorrect passwords.\n\nThe full path from untrusted input to authorized principal is:\n`TCP socket bytes → NetworkReceive → SaslServerAuthenticator → SaslServer.evaluateResponse() → [mechanism parsing + callback handler credential lookup] → SaslServer.getAuthorizationID() → DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder → KafkaPrincipal → Authorizer.authorize()`\n","structuredPatch":[],"originalFile":null}}

An attacker can craft an unsigned JWT with any `sub` claim and authenticate as any principal — a complete authentication bypass.\n\n### Other Gaps\n- PLAIN credentials transmitted in cleartext over SASL_PLAINTEXT (no enforcement of SSL requirement)\n- Partial username oracle for SCRAM: unknown users fail at round 1 (before proof exchange), known users with wrong passwords fail at round 2 (timing difference)\n\n### Existing Mitigations That Work Well\n- 512 KB receive size limit prevents OOM from oversized auth payloads\n- Timing-safe comparisons (`Utils.isEqualConstantTime`, `MessageDigest.isEqual`) for PLAIN and SCRAM\n- SCRAM nonce verification prevents replay attacks\n- Re-authentication principal equality check prevents session hijacking\n- SaslException messages are sanitized (not forwarded to clients)"}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":6817,"cache_read_input_tokens":82809,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":6817,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":1,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"d37f1f18-16fe-45f6-a020-973114f39862","uuid":"d456116d-e2b8-4365-a906-429a053b113b"}
